The parameters p and q refer to the orders of the AR and MA parts, respectively. Both p and q are integers greater than zero. So, it's a bivariate model where the AR part has order p and the MA part has order q.

Now, the pacf. The pacf is the partial autocorrelation, which is like the acf but without the influence of the moving average part. Wait, perhaps I'll look for an example.

Consider ARMA(1,1): X_t = theta X_{t-1} + epsilon_t - psi epsilon_{t-1}.

The acf is theta + -psi, decaying geometrically.

The pacf is the acf of the residuals after removing the AR part, which is epsilon_t - psi epsilon_{t-1}, so the acf is -psi, which is zero after 1 lag.

Thus, for ARMA(1,1), the acf decays geometrically, and the pacf is zero after 1 lag, which is p=1.

1. Start with the given acf of the ARMA(p,q) model, which is a combination of AR(p) and MA(q).

2. For k = p down to 1:

   a. Set ρ_k = ϱ_k

   b. If ϱ_k ≤ ϱ_{k-1}, set ϱ_k = ϱ_{k-1}

3. Set ϱ_0 = 1

4. For k = q down to 1:

   a. Set ϱ_k = min(ϱ_k, ϱ_{k+1})

5. Now, the resulting ϱ_k is the partial autocorrelation function.

First, let me recall what an ARMA(p, q) model is. ARMA stands for Autoregressive Moving Average. It combines both an autoregressive (AR) model and a moving average (MA) model. In AR models, each term is a linear combination of past values, while in MA models, each term is a linear combination of past errors. So, in ARMA(p, q), we have an AR part of order p and an MA part of order q.

The ACF for ARMA(p,q) is given by:

\[

\]

and for \( h > p \),

\[

\]

Where \( \phi_1 \) is the AR coefficient and \( \psi_1 \) is the MA coefficient. So, for lags beyond p, the ACF is a geometric decay with rate \( \psi_1 \). Therefore, unless \( \psi_1 = 0 \), which would make the ACF zero beyond p, but in general, it doesn't become zero. \[
\text{PACF}(h) = \begin{cases}
\phi_1^h & \text{for } h = 1, 2, \dots, p \\
0 & \text{for } h > p
\end{cases}
\]

Yes, that seems correct. An ARMA model is a combination of an autoregressive (AR) model and a moving average (MA) model. The AR part has order p, meaning we have p past lags of the dependent variable in the equation, and the MA part has order q, meaning we use q past lags of the error term to represent the current value.

Now, the question is about the autocorrelation function (acf) and the partial autocorrelation function (pacf) for such a model. Let me remember what each function represents.

On the other hand, the MA model introduces randomness, which affects the acf. For an MA model with order q, the acf will eventually become zero after q lags because the moving average process is a finite memory process.

In AR, the model assumes that the variable can be expressed as a linear combination of its past values, while in MA, it assumes the variable can be expressed as a linear combination of past errors. So, when you combine them, you have both the autoregressive and moving average components.

In ARMA(p,q), p is the order of the autoregressive part, and q is the order of the moving average part. Both p and q are integers greater than zero. That means we can't have a pure AR model (where q=0) or a pure MA model (where p=0) here.

where X_t is the observed time series, ε_t is the white noise (innovations), AR_i are the autoregressive coefficients, and MA_j are the moving average coefficients.

Wait, maybe I need to look at a specific example.

Consider ARMA(1,1): y_t = AR(1) + MA(1).

The AR(1) component is y_t = y_{t-1} + ε_t.

The MA(1) component is y_t = ε_t.

So, combining them, y_t = y_{t-1} + 2ε_t.

This is a random walk with drift, or sometimes called a first-order ARMA model.

The acf for this model is φ_k = E[y_t y_{t-k}] = E[(y_{t-1} + 2ε_t)(y_{t-1} + 2ε_{t-k})]

Expanding this, we have:

φ_k = E[y_{t-1} y_{t-1}] + 2E[y_{t-1} ε_{t-k}] + 2E[ε_t y_{t-1}] + 4E[ε_t ε_{t-k}]

Since ε_t and ε_{t-k} are uncorrelated for k >=1,

φ_k = Var(y_{t-1}) + 4E[ε_t ε_{t-k}]

But for MA(1), E[ε_t ε_{t-k}] is zero for k >1 and equal to 1 for k=1.

Therefore, φ_k = Var(y_{t-1}) + 4*1 if k=1, and φ_k = Var(y_{t-1}) if k>1.

Since y_{t-1} = y_{t-2} + 2ε_{t-1}, recursively, Var(y_{t-1}) = Var(y_{t-2}) + 4E[ε_{t-1}^2] + 4E[ε_{t-1} ε_{t-2}]

But since ε are white noise, Var(y_{t-1}) is a linear function of time, leading to φ_k being a decaying geometric function.

Wait, so in this case, φ_k = Var(y_{t-1}) + 4*1 if k=1, and Var(y_{t-1

Alright, so I need to figure out why the correct answer is option C for the ARMA(p, q) model regarding its autocorrelation function (acf) and partial autocorrelation function (pacf). Let me start by recalling what I know about ARMA models.

An ARMA(p, q) model is a stationary time series model that combines an autoregressive (AR) component of order p with a moving-average (MA) component of order q. The general form is:

\[ X_t = \mu + \sum_{i=1}^p \theta_i X_{t-i} + \sum_{j=1}^q \phi_j \epsilon_{t-j} \]

where \( \mu \) is the mean, \( \theta_i \) are the autoregressive coefficients, and \( \phi_j \) are the moving-average coefficients. The residuals \( \epsilon_{t-j} \) are white noise, meaning they are uncorrelated and have zero mean.

Now, the question is about the properties of the acf and pacf for such a model. The options are:

A. Both acf and pacf decline geometrically.
B. acf declines geometrically and pacf is zero after p lags.
C. acf declines geometrically and pacf is zero after q lags.
D. Both acf and pacf are zero after p and q lags, respectively.

I remember that for AR models, the acf decays geometrically because the process is stationary and autoregressive. For MA models, the acf is white noise, meaning it's zero outside the MA order. For ARMA models, the acf will have a combination of AR and MA characteristics.

So, for an ARMA(p, q) model, the acf should have the decaying exponential behavior from the AR part, but also some white noise after the MA order. Therefore, the acf will decline geometrically for lags up to q, but remain zero beyond that? Wait, no, that doesn't seem right.

Wait, maybe I should think differently. The acf of ARMA(p, q) is the sum of the acf of AR(p) and MA(q). The acf of AR(p) decays geometrically, while the acf of MA(q) is zero beyond lag q. Therefore, the combined acf of ARMA(p, q) will have the decaying geometric behavior up to lag q and then will be the sum of the acfs from both AR and MA.

Wait, but actually, the acf of ARMA(p, q) is the sum of the AR acf and the MA acf. Since the AR part has a decaying geometric acf for all lags, and the MA part has a spike at lags 1 to q and zero beyond. Therefore, the combined acf will have a decaying geometric part from lag 1 to infinity, but also spikes at lags 1 to q. Hmm, that doesn't seem to make sense. Maybe I need to think about the two-sided nature.

Wait, no, the acf for ARMA is actually a combination where the initial lags up to q are a combination of AR and MA effects, but beyond that, the MA part dies out, so the acf becomes purely AR beyond lag q. But AR acf decays geometrically. So actually, the acf for ARMA(p, q) will have a decaying geometric behavior, but with some modification at the initial lags due to the MA component.

Wait, now I'm confused. Maybe I should consider specific lags. For lag 0, it's the variance. For lag 1, it's the autocovariance, which is the sum of the AR coefficient and the MA coefficient.
